         MINAS GERAIS PARTNERSHIPFORDEVELOPMENTPROJECT

                    LOANAND PROGRAMSUMMARY

I                    State of Minas Gerais
IBorrower
 ImplementingAgency  Secretariat of Planningand Management
IAmount              US$170million

                     Fixed Spread U S Dollar Loan with repayment period of 17 years
                     including 3 years of grace with disbursement-linked repayment
                     schedule and level repayments or principal.

                     Commitment fee of 0.85% per annum during the first four years and
                     0.75% per annum thereafter on undisbursed loan amounts,
                     beginning60 days after the loan agreement is signed.

                     Front-endfee of 0.25% of the loan amount, capitalized and financed
                     under the loan.

                     US$lOO million first tranche, US$70 million secondtranche

                     The proposed operation would be a two-tranche development policy
                     loan, with the first tranche (US$lOO million) to be disbursed upon
                     effectiveness and the secondtranche (US$70million) on completion
                     of preset conditions described inthe loan agreement.

                     The operation would support three pillars - fiscal management,
                     public sector management, and private sector development - in two
                     stages, with the initial disbursement made conditional on progress to
                     date at the time of negotiations, and the seconddisbursement tied to
                     clearly defined future goals ineach area.

                     The benefits of the operation can be divided into three levels. At the
                     first level, the operation brings benefits in terms of reinforcing the
                    Fiscal Responsibility Law and Brazil's system of fiscal federal
                    checks and balances. As discussed elsewhere in this document, this
                     system has been fundamental to assuring Brazil's national
                    macroeconomic stability since the Real Plan, and by reinforcing the
                     successful adjustment of Brazil's second largest state, the operation
                    has considerable significance at the national level.

                    Second, within the state itself, the fiscal adjustment combined with
                    improved economic performance will lead to poverty reduction both
                    through higher incomes and through increased spending available
                    for direct poverty-reducing spending, such as education, health, and



                                                                                           2

basic local infrastructure. Work at the national level suggests that
the elasticity of poverty with respect to economic growth inBrazil is
about one, and Minas is unlikely to be a significant exception to this
statistical regularity at the level of the country. Furthermore, there
i s a 25% headcount ratio of poverty in the state. This implies that a
one percent increase in economic growth is likely to reduce poverty
in Minas by about a quarter of a percentagepoint per year, or one
percent every four years.

Third, the improvements in the quality of public management in
Minas will bringbetter service delivery to the poor. Efforts such as
results-based management and performance-based human resource
managementare key inthis regard.

The main risks to the operation are assessedto be the following:

Implementation risks: The risk that the government fails to
implement the planned reform measures and thus fail to meet the
second tranche conditions is deemed to be low. The government
has a proven track record of achievements since taking office in
January 2003.      The second-tranche conditions are designed to
correspond to the current government's own reform targets, to be
met within its current mandate that will end in December 2006.
Mitigationi s thus embeddedin loan design, and will be furthered by
close supervision and ongoing dialogue with the state authorities.

Political risks: There is the risk that unforeseen political events
cause a regime shift at the state level leading to backtracking on
core reforms supported by this loan. This risk is deemed to be low
to moderate, in the sense that experience shows that it i s impossible
to predict political events. However, the present government in
Minas enjoys considerable popular support, in no small part coming
from its perceived seriousness about tackling some of the state's
hardestproblems head on. The only way to mitigate this riskwill be
to continue to build the Bank's constructive and supportive
relationship with the state of Minas Gerais, of which the proposed
loan is the core element.

Fiscal risks: There is the riskthat further economic changes at the
national level - for example related to general price inflation, which
zxerts powerful influences on the state government's financial
obligations to the National Treasury, or renewed economic
instability - cause recent fiscal gains in Minas to be undone 01
reversed. This riski s deemed to be moderate.

                           IBRD PROGRAMDOCUMENTFORA
        PROPOSEDMINAS GERAIS PARTNERSHIPFORDEVELOPMENT
                        DEVELOPMENTPOLICY LOAN(MG-DPL)
            TO THE STATE GOVERNMENTOFMINAS GERAIS,BRAZIL


I. INTRODUCTION

This document describes a proposed two-tranche development policy loan (DPL)of US$170
million to the State of Minas Gerais, Brazil. Minas Gerais (abbreviated to MG or "Minas") i s a
key state client for the World Bank Group in Brazil. The proposed loan would support recent, deep
reforms pursuingfiscal balance, public sector effectiveness, and private sector development in the
state. This would be the first DPLto a Brazilian state.

Minas Gerais is an important state within the Brazilian federation. It is the second most
populous state and the third largest state economy in Brazil. Because of high inequality and
dispersion in regional standards of living within the state, Minas faces a significant challenge of
poverty reduction, with about 5 million people living below a food-based poverty line of about
US$45 per month.

The significance of the current strong performance of MGtranscends the borders of the state
itself.  The Brazilian economy i s only now reaping the rewards of its national efforts in
macroeconomic management, starting with the 1994 Real Plan stabilization, continued with the
stabilization of state debts in 1997, the successful floating of the Real in 1999, the Law of Fiscal
Responsibility in 2000, and the successful transition between the Cardoso and Lula administrations
in2003 and continuation of strongeconomic managementsince then.

A cornerstone of this period of transition has been improved fiscal discipline by the states,
with a noteworthy exception being the threatened default by MG on its debt to the federal
government in 1999. Clearly, the adherence of Brazil's largest states to the principles of fiscal
rectitude that underpin the country's present economic growth, and which are enshrined inthe state
debt agreements signed each year with the NationalTreasury, is absolutely vital to the credibility of
the country in the eyes of investors, both foreign and domestic. The proposed loan would thus
contribute to progress ineconomic managementat the national as well as the state level.




                                                                                                   4


11.      COUNTRY AND STATE CONTEXT

A. RECENTECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS

a) NationalEconomicDevelopments

Nationally, the sound economic policy of the last six years has begun to produce visible returns.
For the first time in two decades, the Brazilian economy is experiencing strong growth with
external balance, fiscal discipline, and low inflation. In 2004, growth was the highest in eight
years, with GDP growth of 4.9 percent (compared to GDP growth of 0.5 percent in 2003,
representing a per capita decline), although tight monetary and fiscal policies and the political crisis
decelerated growth to around 2.5 percent in 2005. Exchange indexed debt and the related Central
Bank of Brazil foreign exchange swaps have fallen from a peak of 41 percent of total debt in 2002
to 2.7 percent as of December 2005. Strong exports led the interest payments on external debt to
exports to fall from the high level of 35 percent in 1999 to less than 15 percent in 2005. Inflation
fell from 12.5 percent in 2002 to 5.7 percent in 2005. For the first time in three decades, the
Brazilian economy has been experiencing a macroeconomic environment that combines reasonable
growth prospects with low inflation and a relatively comfortable external situation.

Unlike previous instances of economic recovery in Brazil, exports have led the current trend, with
the 2004 trade surplus a record US$33.7 billion. Domestic demand has driven the expansion since
mid-2004 without slowing down the rise in investment and exports. Brazil has continued to run a
trade surplus in 2005, US$29 billion as of end August. Strong export performance i s making Brazil
a more open economy: the ratio of trade to GDP has doubled from 15 percent in 1999 to over 30
percent today, reducing vulnerability to external shocks. Sound fiscal and monetary policies and
the performance of the external sector yielded impressive results: for example, sovereign spreads
fell from 2,400 basis points in October 2002 to below 400 basis points in 2004 and have reached
historically low levels (well below 300 basis points) in the early 2006.

Persistence on fiscal discipline has been essential: the primary budget surplus exceeded 5 percent
of GDP in 2004 and reached 4.84 percent of GDP in 2005 (versus the IMF program target 4.25
percent). The primary surplus, higher growth, and lower interest rates reduced public debt in 2005
by 7 percentage points of GDP (from 58 percent to 51 percent). Debt composition has also
improved through the aggressive pursuit of a strong public debt management strategy by the
National Treasury, reducing vulnerability to exchange rate and interest shocks. The government's
strategy to improve the debt composition has resulted in the share of exchange-rate linked bonds
falling from 35 percent in January 2003 to less than 5 percent in July 2005, and the share of fixed-
coupon bonds growing from 2 percent in January 2003 to 20 percent in July 2005. To correct
(albeit partially) the long-term actuarial imbalance of the publicly-funded social security system,
the government enacted a constitutional reform to adjust benefit rules of the pension regime for
retired public servants.

This adjustment has been accomplished through strong revenue increases (the tax burden has
grown from 29 percent of GDP in 1998 to 35 percent in 2004) and by curtailing public investment
(investments by federal government fell from 1.1percent of GDP in 1998 to 0.4 percent in 2003).
This compression of public investment has exacerbated infrastructure bottlenecks, in particular in
roads, railroads, and ports. To sustain growth while re-orienting public finance towards investment
therefore represents the next chapter of Brazil's national economic reforms.




                                                                                                       5

Having reinforced its fiscal and monetary credibility, the government i s therefore now focusing on
improving the quality of public spending, particularly in social and infrastructure sectors. A new
law has been approved to promote public-private partnerships in infrastructure. Inaddition, Brazil
i s participating in the IMF-led pilot program on public investment to improve selection, execution
and monitoring of priority public investment projects.               The government i s also pursuing
microeconomic growth-seeking reforms such as new insolvency law, the reduction of the tax
burden on capital investment, improvements to the laws governing housing credit, and measures to
deepen credit intermediation, particularly to individuals and small firms. There remain large areas
in need of reform where progress will remain slow owing to political factors, most notably tax
reform, additional social security reform, and labor market regulation, which remain important
longer-run objectives.

As mentioned above, Brazil's macroeconomic prospects are currently as good as they have been at
any point since the 198Os, but structural vulnerabilities remain.             A recent IMF assessment
concludedthat:

          "Brazil's impressive economic achievements over the last two years, and the remarkable
          track record of performance under the Stand-By Arrangement, reflected the authorities'
          continued pursuit of strong macroeconomic policies and steady progress with structural
          reforms. Helpedby a generally supportive external environment, these policies have led to
          a significant transformation of the economy, which has resulted in a strong economic
          recovery, income and employment gains, a declining public debt burden, and rising
          international reserves."

The assessment added, however, that "public debt was still high and sensitive to global financial
conditions," and stressed"the need to persevere with structural reforms in order to boost long-term
growth prospects, critical to addressingpersistent poverty and inequality."'

Debt sustainability analyses, by both the Bank and the IMF, have shown Brazil's sovereign debt
dynamics to be robustly sustainable under a wide variety of assumptions and stress tests. An in-
depth World Bank study of Brazil's debt sustainability carried out at the height of the 2002 crisis
concluded that public debt was sustainable and would decline under most plausible assumptions
about the steady state of key economic variables.2 More recent IMF analyses of Brazilian debt
sustainability have repeatedly come to the same conclusions.

As mentioned above, one of the keys to the good performance of Brazil's consolidated fiscal
accounts3in recent years has been the ability of the federal government to impose fiscal discipline
on the state governments since the 1997 state debt bailout. This has been achieved via debt
agreements with each state (renewed annually) combined with the legal sanctions contained in the
Fiscal Responsibility Law. The effects are shown in Figure 1, which illustrates the contribution of
the states and municipalities to the consolidated primary surplus over time. This key role of sub-
national fiscal discipline means that the proposed operation has important ramifications for national
debt sustainability, by supporting and strengthening the adjustment efforts of a major state with a
large debt stock owed to the federal government.

2005.

b) State Economic Developments

Minas Gerais i s sometimes called a microcosm of Brazil. While its income level i s close to
Brazil's national average (ranked 10 out of 27), Minas contains a poor Northeastern region with the
same conditions of extreme poverty, subsistence agriculture, semi-arid climate and intermittent
drought experienced in the rest of the Northeastern SertZo. At the other extreme, the state contains
some of Brazil's most dynamic industrial areas (e.g., the Trigngulo Mineiro) and the metropolis of
Belo Horizonte.

The population of Minas in the 2000 census was 17.9 million (10.5 percent of the national total),
about half that of the State of Sgo Paulo (37.0 million). The state's total income in 2001 was
R$113.5 billion, 9.5 percent of national income, smaller only than SBo Paulo (33.4 percent) and Rio
de Janeiro (12.3 percent), and giving Minas Gerais an economy slightly larger than that of Peru.

Economic Growth and Investment Climate

Lags in data availability make it hard to give an up-to-date assessment of economic trends at the
state level, but history suggests that Minas has probably performed in line with the national
economy in recent years. Throughout the late 1980s and the 1990s the growth rate of the MG state
economy was very close to that of Brazil. For example, after the Real Plan, for the period 1995-
1999, Brazilian GDP grew at 2.6 percent per annum, while in Minas this growth rate was 2.9
percent (MG population growth has also been close to the national rate). The exception to this
representative performance was the period 1999-2002. During this period, Minas' real incomes
declined by 0.6 percent a year while national real per capita growth was low but still positive (0.5
percent per annum over four years).4


 These statistics are calculated combining the most recent IMF GDP deflators for Brazil to IBGE regional
GDP statistics for the period 1999-2002.


                                                                                                      7

The economic structure of Minas still reflects the state's historical roots in mining, with relative
strength in industrial chemicals, iron and steel, agroindustry, and more recently in electrical and
electronic goods. The state is geographically expansive and has a strong agricultural base. In the
standard breakdown of state income calculated by the Brazilian Geographical and Statistical
Institute (IBGE), Minas has slightly higher concentrations than the national average in industry and
agriculture, and lower concentrations in services. Despite higher growth in agricultural output than
in other sectors, however, falling prices drove down the agricultural share in the state economy
(from 15 percent in 1989 to 9 percent in 1999). Within manufacturing, the most significant sectors
are metallurgical (25 percent of manufacturing), foodstuffs (16 percent), chemicals (14 percent),
and transportation equipment (13 percent).

Although Minas is an important exporting state, its exports remain concentrated in primary goods.
Minas lost ground throughout the 1990s to other exporting states, notably Rio Grande do Sul and
ParanB, which made gains in the automotive sectors. Although Minas accounted for important
shares of Brazil's exports in extractive mineral (40 percent of the nationaltotal) and metallurgy (27
percent), its overall share of exports fell from 14.6 percent in 1990 to 10.4 percent in 2001e5It i s
also noteworthy that Minas is landlocked - its competitiveness i s therefore sensitive to logistics
costs, which have worsened as investment in road maintenance has fallen to historically low
levek6 World Bank sector analysis suggests roads inMinas are inpoor condition even by national
standards. This is confirmed by data from the Brazilian Investment Climate Survey (ICs) collected
by the World Bank in 2003 (Figure 2), which records an average number of days lost to transport
failures for enterprises in Minas (1.77) comparable to states in the more remote Center-West and
Northernregions of Brazil.

Economic Management

A main driver of MG's poor performance in comparison with the rest of Brazil over the period
1999-2002 was the uncertain investment climate that had been brought about by several events
related to government policy includingthe threat of default on the external debt and the debt with
National Treasury, which the federal government aggressively resolved by securing the legal right
to withdraw the scheduled payments from earmarked tax flows to the state, effectively neutralizing
the state's threat, and the state government's failure to curb its own spending sufficiently to create
confidence inthe state economy's future stability.

Brazil as a whole entered a recession in 2002 as uncertainties surrounding Argentina's economy
and the outcome of the presidential election wrought havoc with investor confidence in sovereign
debt sustainability. At the same time as the Lula government sought to impose its own brand of
economic discipline and turn around a difficult situation, Minas Gerais was also attempting to
rebuild its own credibility through a difficult adjustment inthe public accounts.

Upon assuming power on January 1, 2003, the current state government launched a managerial
shake-up that aimed to get public spending under control and lay the foundations for improved
public-sector management. Among other actions, the program reduced by six the number of state
secretariats, cut non-salary personnel costs, improved government purchasing systems, defined the
integrated state development plan, stepped up its revenue efforts, and started the process of results-
basedmanagement of government programs and human resources.

The MG government was effective in realizing its stated priority in`2003 of regaining financial
control of the state apparatus. After the exchangerate shock of 2002 increasedthe consolidated net
debt to 297 percent of net current revenue, this ratio fell to 250 percent in 2003 and to 225 percent
in 2004. The exchange rate appreciation of 2005 fostered an acceleration of the reduction path of
indebtedness: information up to December 2005 indicates that this ratio fell to 203 percent, very
close to the Fiscal Responsibility Law ceiling for the consolidated net debt to net current revenue
of 200 percent.

Deepening of this adjustment will not be easy and relies on detailed legal reforms and fundamental
changes in the state model of public sector management. As at the federal level, a major challenge
i s to control growth of public expenditures. In 2003, "rigid" state expenditures (personnel, interest
payments, constitutional transfers, and debt amortizations) still represented 82 percent of total
expenditure (down from more than 85 percent until 2000). Even the remaining 18 percent are not
fully discretionary. Nonetheless, the first stage of a difficult transition has been accomplished, with
the state government able to announce in late 2004 that, for the first time since the Real Plan
stabilization, it would balance its budget while meeting all its financial obligations for the fiscal
year.

Poverty Reduction

Poverty in MG broadly reflects national levels. Using a national food-based poverty line for
household per-capita income of about R$146 (US$54) per month, Minas has a poverty headcount
ratio of about 25 percent, implying a poverty headcount of about 5 million. Using an extreme
poverty line (about half the poverty line), Minas has an extreme poverty headcount ratio of 8
percent (about 1.5 million). Poverty has been declining in Minas since the Real Plan, while in the
Southeast as a whole it has increased since 1998 (Figure 3), mainly owing to risingpoverty in Sgo




                                                                                                      9

Paulo. Between 1997 and 2003, extreme poverty in Minas Gerais fell from 9.8 percent to 8.1
percent, a decline of 17 percent. Poverty counts will almost certainly be lower for 2004.

Just as for Brazil, these averages mask considerable regional variation within MG. Regional
poverty rates in Minas for 1999 varied between lows of 10percent in the TriSngulo Mineiro and 12
percent in the Central Mineiro, and highs of 46 percent in the Vale do Mucuri, 50 percent in the
Norte de Minas, and 56 percent in the Vale do Jequitinhonha, the latter equal to rates recorded in
Brazil's poorest state^.^

This variation is reflected at the municipal level. The Funda@o Jog0 Pinheiro, the state's public
statistics and research institute, has calculated municipal human development indices (HDI) based
on census data for 1991 and 2000. Setubinha (inthe Vale do Mucuri) has the lowest HDIinMinas
in 2000 (0.45), reflecting 23 percent illiteracy (ages 18-24) and infant mortality of 61 (one-year
definition, per 1000 live births).' At the other end of the spectrum Belo Horizonte has an HDI of
0.84 - equal to Sgo Paulo - with near complete literacy (ages 18-24) and infant mortality of 27
(smaller municipalities in MG have rates as low as 11). Outside the Amazon, Minas Gerais has the
highest dispersion of municipal welfare indicators in Brazil.'

111.     MAIN DEVELOPMENT CHALLENGES AND THE GOVERNMENT
         STRATEGY

A. FISCAL TURNAROUND AND REGAINING PRIVATE SECTOR CONFIDENCE AS
MAINDEVELOPMENT CHALLENGES

Development challenges of Minas Gerais are multifold.              However, the behavior of the state
economy in recent years pointed to the need to restore fiscal sustainability and regain private sector
confidence in the government as top priorities. Indeed, the state's own major economic study
identified lack of economic dynamism and competitiveness as a key problem, and attributed it to
the incapacity of the public sector to be a main promoter of economic and social development."

         The public sector (a ma`quina pu'blica) of Minas Gerais, is close to total inoperability,
         constrained by debts, with little expenditure flexibility and limited financial capacity.

Consequences of the ineffective state were clearly felt in the behavior of private economic agents.
Although the annualized growth rate of the Minas economy in 1990-99 (2.86%) was somewhat
higher than the national average (2.46%), by the end of the 1990s, there was a sign that investors
were losing interest in Minas as a destination of their investment activities.              By 2000, the
announced investments declined to less than half the level registered in 1996." While for Brazil
total announced private investments rose by 8.5 percent in 1999 and 9.7 percent in 2000, in Minas
announced investments fell by 29.1 percent and 19.3 percent, respectively, in these two years. In
2000, the amount of foreign direct investments in Minas represented 13 percent of the national
total, nearly half the level registered (25%) in the 1970s.

It was against the backdrop of these self-diagnoses that the government prepared its overall
development strategies as documented in the state's long-term development plan for 2003-2020,
PMDI(Plano Mineiro de Desenvolvimento Zntegrado) and the state's multi-year expenditure plan,
PPAG (Plano Plurianual de A@ Governamental) 2004-2007.'2 These plans' overall objective is
to improve the state's human development index - "to make Minas the best Brazilian state to live
in" - through a strategy built upon three pillars: (i)       reorganizing and modernizing state public
administration;    (ii)promoting sustainable social and economic development; and (iii)
reinvigorating Minas Gerais politically beyond its borders, in Brazil and overseas. Within the
broad range of policy initiatives the government i s developing, the most relevant for this proposed
operation are (a) achievement of fiscal balance; (b) reorganization and modernization of the state
public administration; and (c) sustainable economic development through promotion of private
sector investments.




loMinas Gerais do S6culo XXI: Una VisHodo Novo Desenvolvimento, Banco de Desenvolvimento de Minas
Gerais (BDMG),Belo Horizonte, Ron Editora, 2002.
l1Minas Geraisdo S6culo XXI: 0 Ponto de Partida, Vol. 1,p. 46.
  The PMDI and the PPAG were born from a lengthy diagnostic exercise carried out by the Minas Gerais
Development Bank (BDMG) toward the end of the previous administration's tenure. That study - "Minas
Gerais in the 21" Century" - provides an in depth analysis o f the predicament the state found itself in 2002
and the path by which it had arrived there. It is worth recognizing that the Minas study has been a valuable
resource for the Bank team in preparing this report.

B. GOVERNMENT RESPONSES

a) FiscalBalance13

Reestablishing fiscal balance and ensuring fiscal sustainability has clearly been a top priority of the
current Minas administration. Faced with extremely adverse fiscal conditions, the government
initiated a set o f bold adjustment measures upon assuming power in January 2003. These measures
have reversed the unsustainable trend that its fiscal accounts had taken in the previous four-year
period. An increasingtrend in expenditures has been curbed through tight control of personnel and
operating costs. On the revenue side, measures to improve the efficiency of tax collection have
also shown results. The combination of control over current expenditures with enhancedefficiency
of revenue collection has allowed the state government to achieve an impressive fiscal turnaround
with increased current savings, allowing a recovery in investment spending, which, if sustainedand
well-managed, i s expected to have apositive impact on economic growth.

The results have been immediate and impressive. Minas i s now generating substantial positive
fiscal balances. Between 2001 and 2003, the primary balance increased from 0.8 percent to 7.5
percent of the net current revenues (NCR), the current balance increased from -2.8 percent (which
worsened to -5.0 percent in 2002) to 2.8 percent of NCR, and the nominal balance increased from -
9.6 percent to 0.7 percent of NCR. This fiscal turnaround happenedin a period of severe economic
stagnation, which was embodied in a 7-percent fall in revenues. With reduced revenues, cutting
expenditures was the only way to improve fiscal balances in 2003. Total spendingdecreasedby 10
percent that year.

In 2004, this adjustment was deepened. The state government obtained a primary surplus of 15.5
percent of NCR, generated current account savings of 14 percent and generated a nominal balance
of 8.6 percent of NCR. Differently from the previous year, the achievement of the improved fiscal
stance owed more to strengthening of revenue collection, although the government kept under
check growth of current expenditures. The strong recovery of the economy also consolidated the
adjustment, allowing a 12-percent increase in fiscal revenues. Official figures for 2005 have not
been released yet, but the preliminary data indicate that the State has maintained strong fiscal
performance in 2005.

This initial emphasis on adjustment by expenditure reduction (in 2003) is a noteworthy feature that
places Minas' approach in contrast to the federal government's fiscal adjustment efforts since
1998. The federal efforts have also been successful in maintaining aggregate fiscal discipline but
have had to rely primarily on revenue increases given the extremely high level of expenditure
rigidity that precludes cuts in most expenditure items, at least in the short run. Experiences
elsewhere show that fiscal adjustments are more sustainable when they are based on reductions in
current expenditures. Indeed, in 2004 the MG government allowed the current expenditures to
increase somewhat, but still kept their level below the 2002 level, while registering an 11-percent
increase in revenues through a series of improvements to tax administration. Combined with the
launch of a comprehensive results-based public management reform, Minas' approach clearly
shows the state government's concern about the quality of fiscal adjustments.

Medium-term impacts of these adjustment measures are expected to be quite positive and
sustainable. Projection exercises for 2005-20 14 undertaken for the loan preparation (reported in
full inAnnex 5) depict afavorable evolution inkey fiscal and financial indicators, and indicatethat
continuity in fiscal management will ensure debt sustainability. Sensitivity analysis nonetheless
shows that this predicted positive trend depends on continued improvements in the efficiency of tax


                                                                                                    13

revenue collection and perseverancein the tight control of current expenditures. Positive prospects
also depend on economic growth exceeding population growth, a reasonablehypothesis.

Risk analysis shows that the most important underlying source of vulnerability is the state's high
level of indebtedness. High debt service will continue to exert pressure on public finances. This
indicates the importance of continuing with tight control over expenditures and increasing revenue
collection, which are the main variables under the government control and which the government i s
managing quite effectively. The exchange rate constitutes another major risk factor for debt
sustainability because 10 percent of the state's debt stock i s indexed to exchange rate and 90% to
the General Price Index (IGP) which i s very sensitive to changes in exchange rate. On the other
hand, this also implies that an additional external credit operation such as the one proposed will not
generate additional exchange rate vulnerability. While a logical measure to mitigate the exchange
rate risk would be to improve the debt composition by reducing the share of the IGP-indexed debt,
the state's ability to implement such conversions is limited because most of these debts are fixed in
the bilateral debt refinancing agreement between the state and the National Treasury. The state
government does have a strategy to improve the debt profile on the margin by retiring high-cost
debts such as those arising from the judicial rulings against the state. The proposed operation
would support such measures.

I Box 1: Brazilian Fiscal Federalism and State Debt Agreements with the National Treasury                            I
  After stabilization under the Real Plan in 1994, Brazilian state governments had lost a key tool for balancing
~


  their accounts: delaying payments in a high-inflation environment. The result was that most states ran up debt
  at unsustainable rates for the next few years. By 1997, the situation had become untenable, and the federal
  government entered into a negotiation to take on states' debts in exchange for future controls on their abilities
  to incur new liabilities. Today's arrangements between the states and the National Treasury, embodied in
  annual debt agreements signed by state governors and reinforced by the 2000 FiscalResponsibility Law, i s the
  result of that renegotiation.

  Debt servicing on liabilities incurred up until the debt agreements were signed - "intra-limit'' debt servicing -
  i s capped at a percentage of the state government's net current revenues (NCR: defined as total revenues
  minus obligatory transfers to municipalities), usually 13 percent. Importantly, interest payments above this
  ceiling are capitalized as intra-limit state debt, implying that a state's debt ratio may be on a rising path while
  its interest payments are not. Moreover, the interest rate on intra-limit debt i s a fixed percentage spreadabove
  a general price index (the IGP-DI), whose behavior in recent years has been erratic, often somewhat arbitrarily
  penalizing states such as Minas Gerais with high existing debt burdens. This fixed percentageis 6 percent for
  states whose debts are below certain target levels (generally 200 percent of NCR); 7 percent on debts above
  this ceiling.

  The use of a general price index that has been relatively volatile in comparison with consumer prices has
  meant that in years when this price index rose rapidly, heavily indebted states saw their debt-to-NCR ratios
  rise uncontrollably. There i s nothing these states could realistically have done to avoid this. This implies that
  when carrying out debt sustainability analysis for states, the extent of debt build-up owing to this form of
  indexation should be taken into account in sensitivity analyses.


 b) ReorganizationandModernizationof the State PublicAdrninistrati~n`~

 A main thrust of Minas' fiscal adjustment efforts was to make the public sector leaner and regain
 its capacity to finance key development projects, while leveraging private investments to stimulate
 growth. An important corollary to this approach has been aggressive efforts to transform the state
 public administration into more efficient and results-oriented institution. Deficiencies in the state's
 public administration had been diagnosed in the BDMG study cited above, and included the
 fol10wing:'~


       0    Fragmented administrative structure as a result of years of uncoordinated institutional
            creations (e.g., the administrative structure of the agriculture sector, which might have been
            adequate inthe 1970s-80s, did not reflect the increased role of the private sector)
       0    Inflexible human resource management that caused inefficient deployment of public
            servants (e.g., in the education sector, teachers used to be hired into very specific jobs and
            locations, which made it virtually impossible to re-assign them from one school to another,
            let alone from one municipality to another; nearly 2,000 teachers were working outside the
            classrooms due to the reduced student enrollment)
       0    Ineffective salary policy that could not be used to motivate and retain specialized staff
            (e.g., the Government has had a policy of permitting into the public service graduates of the
            government-run School of Government, but a number of these graduates would leave the
            public service after a few years given the inhospitable working conditions in the public
            sector)


 l4 See Annex 9 for additional details.
 l5 Minus Geuris do Se`culoXXI: 0 Poder Pliblico. Vol. 9.


                                                                                                                 15

    0    Weak planning system (e.g., government planning could not ensure that priority objectives
         would guide government activities, and instead, availability of cash resources and political
         expediency dictated resource allocations)
    0    Delayed development of E-government initiatives (comparedto the federal government and
         some states)

To address these deficiencies, the MG government launched a coherent plan to strengthen its
planning and managementfunctions. The secretariats of planning and administration were merged
into a single Secretariat of Planning and Management (SEPLAG) to provide clear political and
technical leadership inthis endeavor.

To revive the government's centralplanning function, SEPLAG adopteda results-oriented strategic
planning approach in which the state's key priorities are operationalized through 31 "structural
projects," collectively called the GERAES program.16 Creation of these "structural projects"
brings duel benefits of clarifying the government's policy priorities and facilitating strategic
management of these priority projects with special arrangements. Accountability for management
of these "structural projects" has been clarified with designation of a manager for each project, and
definition of a set of performance indicators.

These projects together accounted for 26 percent of the total investment budget in 2004 and 39
percent in 2005. This selectivity allows the government to guarantee sufficient budgetary funding
to these projects, and thus facilitate their smooth implementation. Besides the selectivity, key to
funding predictability i s the special legal measure the State Legislative Assembly approved to
guarantee budgetary funding to the "structural projects." The measure limits the Assembly's own
ability to amend budget allocations to these projects. A result i s an improvement in predictability
of budget allocation within and between fiscal years, which allows project managers to plan their
execution with a medium-term perspective and thus minimize costs associated with a typical stop-
and-go cycle of budget execution in Brazil. Government data show that execution of the capital
investment projects belonging to the GERAES program was superior to the execution performance
of the other capital investment pr0je~ts.l~

Along with the impressive fiscal turnaround, the current MG government is perhaps best known for
its comprehensive public management reform, called Choque de GestHo (Management Shock).
Choque de GestHo comprises a series of results-oriented and/or efficiency-enhancing measures to

improve management of personnel, logistics, and performance of the government agencies and staff

SEPLAG is spearheading parallel efforts to strengthen the merit base of the government human
resources and their performance orientations, while controlling the inertial growth of personnel
expenditures. Specific measures adopted include restructuring of the public sector career system
and rationalization of non-salary monetary benefits to public servants.       The government is
recruiting new staff in areas of need, based on the new system, and i s also mapping existing staff
into the new career structures.

Choque de GestZo also includes administrative simplifications intended to make government
operations more efficient and its services more easily accessible to citizens and private firms.
Administrative processes that have been reengineered include the state's real-estate portfolio
management, personnel management and administration of civil servant retirement benefits, state
budgeting and financial programming, medical supplies ordering, prison management, hospital
management, tax collection, and contract management. The government has estimated the savings
from these improvements over the period 2003-2004 to be R$2.1 billion, with further gains of the
order of R$1.1 billion expected for 2005. For comparison, the state government's net current



                                                                                                 17

revenue (NCR) in 2003 was R$13.4 billion, implying that efficiency gains from "Excellence in
Management" have been of the order of eight percent of NCR.

 Choque de GestLTo has included centrally coordinated efforts to increase economy and efficiency in
government purchases. To this end, SEPLAG adopted an aggressive use of "pregiio" (reverse
auction) to purchase standard supplies, developed a single registry of suppliers (including a registry
of suppliers barred from participating in public procurement), and adopted mandatory use of the
Integrated System of Administration of Materials and Services of the State (SIAD). With these
(and other) measures, the Government has reportedly obtained savings of 32 percent of the cost of
government purchases and procurement, totaling R$2 billion a year. The Government plans to
extend the use of e-pregiio to further improve the economy in government procurement, and
complete a number of SIAD modules that are still not fully operational.

Finally, the Minas government i s exploring innovative ways of delivering public services through
expanded use o f e-government tools and partnerships with non-governmental institutions in two
modalities. One i s the Public-PrivatePartnership (PPP) as discussedbelow, and the other is the use
of OSCIP (UrganizupZo du Sociedude Civil de Interesse Pziblico).'' Other reformist states such as
Cear6 and SFio Paulo are also experimenting with the OSCIP model of social service delivery with
promising initial results.

c) Sustainable Economic Developmentlg

At the beginning of the current administration, one of the government's principal preoccupations
with regard to competitiveness of the state economy was the behavior of private investments. The
Investment Climate Survey conducted by the Bank reveals the investment climate in the state of
Minas relative to other states and to Brazil as a whole. Mineiro business respondents to the survey
ranked as the five most serious constraints to operating a business the tax rates, the cost of capital,
macroeconomic uncertainty, regulatory uncertainty and corruption. The survey reveals that in
terms of these subjective rankings, Minas i s representative of Brazil as a whole (see Figure 4,
which includes the Southeast region, in which Minas Gerais, Siio Paulo, and Rio de Janeiro were
sampled): the differences between Minas responses and Brazil as a whole in the sample is
insignificant.

Promotion of private investments in export-oriented sectors is a high policy priority for Minas.
Nonetheless, most of the constraints identified by the business sector in Minas are outcomes of
policies at the federal level, over which the state government has little to no control. These refer to
much of the tax burden (In 2004, the states accounted for approximately 24% of total federal
revenue), cost of the capital (subject to the national monetary policy and financial sector
regulation), part of the regulatory uncertainty and macro instability. The federal government has
been addressing some of these constraints through its microeconomic reform agenda, which the
Bank supports with its Sustainable and Equitable Growth DPL series. What remains under the
state's control includes measures to re-establish its "macro" (ie., fiscal) stability, control
corruption and reduce regulatory uncertainty in policies and services that are directly administered
by the state itself.

It i s precisely in those latter areas where the Minas government has made important progress in the
last 3 years. Apart from the adoption of a growth-oriented fiscal policy, which has enabled
recuperation of the state government's fiscal capacity to finance needed public investments in
critical areas such as logistics, the main components of the government strategy in support of
private sector development in Minas Gerais are: 1) The "structural project" Competitive Business
of Minas (which includes a special line of credit and programs to lessen the impact of government
bureaucracy in the private sector: Facilita Minas and MinasFAcil), 2) Simples Minas (which i s a
simplified tax treatment for small and medium-sized firms), 3) transformation of the old agency for
industrial development as a new investment promotion agency, the Institute of Integrated
Development of Minas Gerais (INDI), and 4) The Public-Private Partnerships (in which Minas
Gerais has taken a lead role in Brazil and it i s expected to increase substantially private sector
investment in infrastructure) to leverage private financing and to supplement the state's still limited
fiscal capacity.

Within the umbrella framework of a "structural project" Empresa Mineira Competitiva
(Competitive Business of Minas), the government has been developing a series of measures to
streamline government-business transactions and facilitate opening of new businesses and support
growth of medium and small enterprises. The project includes a line of credit for small and micro
enterprises, which aims at financing modernization of processes and enhancing their
competitiveness and productivity for the production of goods and services.                            Reforms to
administrative procedures have been spearheaded by the program Facilita Minas which provides



                                                                                                             19

potential business entrants with both a one-stop shop physical facility called Minas Fdcil and a
web-based mechanism to allow potential investors to inquire about various required procedures in
areas such as tax and firm registration, environmental regulations, etc.

Minas was the first Brazilian state to pass its own PPP Law2' (the federal law governing PPPs -
important to support the state law - was approved by congress in December 2004). Having created
a specialized unit within the Secretariat of Economic Development to manage PPP projects, the
state has defined five pilot PPP projects, includingthe rehabilitation and widening of the BR-050
highway linking Belo Horizonte to S2o Paul0 and the provision of basic sanitation in poor
municipalities.21 Certain aspects of the Minas PPP law are worth emphasizing, since they aim to
reinforce the fiscal benefits of the initiative and reduce fiscal risks undertaken by the government:
first, the law limits PPPs to projects whose objectives are pre-existing in the Integrated
Development Plan of the state (PMDI); second, PPPs are required by the law to demonstrate value-
for-money gains over the alternative of direct public sector provision. The state government has
finalized detailing of the first project, and i s in a process to determine the specific institutional
design of the PPP guaranteemechanism.

Five of the 31 "structural projects" are designed to address infrastructure bottlenecks in transport
and logistics. These include a project to link road networks between municipalities within the
state,22 which was the first "structural project" to sign a results agreement, and another one to
reinvigorate tourism to Minas around the historic theme of the "Estrudu Real" ("The Royal
Highway").

Box 2: Estrada Real

The Estrada Real is the old cobblestone road that linked Rio de Janeiro with the precious metal and gemstone
mining areas of central and northern Minas Gerais. The highway passes through some of Minas' most
historically significant areas, some of which have declined seriously in economic terms since the days it was
built.  The project therefore has both cultural and social importance for the state, representing both a
renaissance of Mineiro historical pride and historical awareness and a focus of resources on poor
municipalities.   A prerequisite for increased tourism is improved infrastructure for both transport and
sanitation, and the project incorporates significant investments in both these sectors: 2004 saw the completion
of 194km of access road paving and improved sanitation brought to 78 municipalities. In addition, three
technical training centers have been set up to provide necessary skills.

IV.     BANK SUPPORTTO THE GOVERNMENT STRATEGY

A. ANALYTICAL UNDERPINNINGS

A recent quality assessment of the World Bank's programof analytical work in Brazil praised this
work for its strategic relevance and overall quality. The proposed operation is built on a number of
reports by the Bank and the state government.

Among prior Bank work, "Brazil: The New Growth Agenda"23underlined the reforms needed to
improve the investment climate in Brazil and the interplay between the macroeconomic and
microeconomic domains, a key feature of the proposed operation. This knowledge base has
recently been deepened by the completion of the Brazilian Investment Climate Assessment,
recently shared with government. The Report on federal multiyear planning (PPA) informed the
Bank's analysis of the public management reforms underway in M i n a ~ Country level fiduciary
                                                                            . ~ ~
work (e.g., CFAA) all provided insight into the state's performance on financial and fiduciary
aspects.

A significant amount of technical analysis has informed loan preparation at the state level,
augmenting and complementing the above-mentioned work at the national level.                Brazilian
consultants provided detailed assessments of (and support to) the state's efforts in the areas of
results-based management, financial management (Annex 6), debt sustainability, and procurement.
The Bank also assessedthe state's proposals inthe area of social security reform.

Finally, the need for a formal Bank State Economic Memorandum, as have been carried out in
several Northeastern states to inform the Bank's assistance strategies, was obviated by the in-depth
analysis that the then incoming state government had carried out in 2002.25

B. THEPROPOSEDPARTNERSHIP FORDEVELOPMENT

The proposed operation would be a two-tranche development policy loan, with the first tranche
(US$lOO million) payable upon effectiveness and the second tranche (US$7Om) due on completion
of preset conditions described in the loan agreement.

The operation would support three pillars - fiscal management, public sector management, and
private sector development - in two stages, with the initial disbursement made conditional on
progress to date at the time of negotiations, and the second disbursement tied to clearly defined
future goals in each area.

Disbursement of the first tranche would recognize the track record the state government has built
up since January 2003 in turning around the difficult fiscal situations, beginning to implement
cutting-edge public sector management reforms, and initiating a set of institutional reforms to
improve the state's investment climate. Disbursement of the second tranche would signal that the
state had sustained fiscal discipline and achieved important incremental progress in the public
management reforms and improvements in the state's investment climate. In the fiscal area, the
state would have consolidated budget balance over multiple years and state public debt would be on
a downward path, without further compromising public investment or raising tax burdens. In

A.     Ensuring FiscalBalance

1) Compliance with the federal fiscal framework

The strong fiscal adjustments in Brazil since 1998 have been underpinned by a set of robust legal
and administrative mechanisms to encourage sub-national governments to comply with the national
fiscal adjustment goals. Two of the most important mechanisms are the bilateral debt refinancing
agreement between the National Treasury (STN) and each state government, including Minas
Gerais, and the Law of Fiscal Responsibility (LRF). Since 2003, the Minas government has
managed to meet the set of performance targets specified by both these mechanisms, including the
steady increase in the primary surplus (from R$665 million in 2002 to R$1,263 million in 2004)
and the reduction in the ratio of personnel expenditures to net current revenues (RCL) to the level
stipulated in the agreement of the state with the STN Cfrom 71% in 2002 to 61% in 2004).26 These
achievements constitute conditions for the first-tranche release.

For the second tranche, the state government has committed itself to achieving the level of primary
surplus in 2005 as agreed to in its agreement with the STN (R$1,596 million), and to maintaining
the ratio of personnel expenditures to 60% or less of the RCL. These key fiscal variables are under
the government's direct control and thus are the best indicators of the maintenance of the sound
fiscal policy during the duration of the operation. Barring negative movements in other variables
that affect the debt dynamics of the state (e.g., exchange rate), these measures are expected to
contribute positively to improvements in key macro indicators including the debt burden, as
measuredinterms of the debt to net current revenue ratio.

2) Reduction of the public debt burden

As the debt sustainability analysis showed, one of the major risksto debt sustainability is the sheer
size of the current stock of debt and the high financial costs involved with some of the debts. The
state government has been aggressively pursuing a strategy of reducing financial costs of the debt
stock. One of the concrete measures taken so far i s to reduce signifcantlyfloating debts (pending
payments) owed toproviders of goods and services (by R$441 million in 2003 and R$611 in 2004).
Such floating debts would have arisen from irresponsible expenditure management, whereby
purchase commitments were made beyond the government's capacity to pay. The cancellation of
these pending payments (debts) to providers of goods and services to the state government has
reportedly brought about an additional benefit of lowering the cost of government procurement.
The assurance of timely payment removes private suppliers' incentives to try to cover the cost of
delayed payments with inflation of biddingprices.

Most of the state's debt is now owed to the federal government through the aforementioned debt
refinancing agreement, and thus i s not subject to financial management for the purpose of cost
reduction. However, the state government intends to pay off some of the remaining debts prior to



26The ratio of personnel expenditures over the net current revenue is one of the key indicators for monitoring
governments' fiscal performance according to the Fiscal Responsibility Law (LRF). LRF, through an
associated Federal Senate Resolution, sets as the upper limit for states 60 percent of the net current revenues
for personnel expenditures. Given the slight difference in the methodology used to calculate the personnel
expenditures in the state-STN agreement for fiscal adjustment, which predates LRF,the Bank has agreed with
the Minas government to use the STN method for the purpose of calculating the personnel expenditurehet
current revenue ratio, but the spirits of both arrangements are fully consistent with each other, and the resultant
estimates differ very little.


                                                                                                                23

the release of the second tranche. The government's debt management plan calls for it to retire at
least R$100 million worth of the most costly debtsfrom the stock of the consolidated debt and/or
the outstandingfloating debt. The Bank will continue to discuss with the state government its debt
management strategy to support implementation of this action.

3) Modernization of the State Revenue Sub-secretariat

The long term aim of the state in the area of tax collection is to generate sufficient stable revenue
from its own sources (Le., net of transfers from the federal government, which in 2003 represented
approximately 14 percent of total revenues) to smooth the state government's spending over the
economic cycle and thus make the state's fiscal policy counter-cyclical.

A significant step forward has been the establishment of "matrix management" of tax collection in
the state revenue service (SRE). This brings the state the ability to cross-check tax claims from
different information sources, increasing its ability to enforce tax collection and detect evasion. To
further sharpen a results focus, SRE has signed a results agreement (as part of the SEPLAG-led
Choquede Gest6o) to formalize its performance management scheme, and installed a computerized
information management systemfor tax payer registration and information and for collection of
tax on transfers due to death and grants of any goods or rights under the Tax Administration
Modernization Project (PROMAT). These measures have translated into a 6.2 percent real rise in
ICMS receipts in 2003 over 2002 (versus 3.9 percent nationally) and an estimated 12.2 percent real
rise in ICMS receipts in 2004 over 2003 (versus 8.9 percent nati~nally).~~These gains owe mostly
to improvements in efficiency (as opposed to economic growth or changes in the tax code).
Collection efficiency has also increased on the other taxes raised by the state government. These
efficiency gains have come from modernized systems, the above-mentioned matrix management,
and improved human-resource managementin the state revenue service. The long-term measure of
impact i s the rate of growth of the state's own tax revenues.

For the second tranche release, the government i s committed to installing ut least seven modules of
the State's Integrated Tax Administration System (Sistema Integrado de ArrecadapZo da Receita
Estadual, SIARE), with the inclusion of the following modules on tax collection: the taxpayers'
fiscal balance (tax debt and credit to the government), the special regime of control and
supervision, the authorized fiscal document control, the automatic issuance of tax receipts for small
firms and independent producers, andelectronic data management.

B.     Excellence inPublic Management

1) Optimization of human resource management

Having achieved important financial savings from rationalization of the wage bill, the government
i s keen to ensure that the financial gains achieved through control of the aggregate wage bill are not
easily reversed by imprudent future decisions.           To this end the Government has adopted a
centralized decision-making structure to ensure new hiring decisions are made with present and
future fiscal implications in mind. In parallel, SEPLAG has centralized management of payrolls,
including of the Indirect Administration entities (e.g., public enterprises), to gain full control over


2' ICMS (Impost0sobre Operapies relativas d Circula@o de Mercadoriase sobre Prestaqces de Sewiqos de

receipts, 70 percent of own current revenue, and 60 percent of total current revenue.


                                                                                                         24

salary payments and standardize criteria for concession of benefits to public servants. A key
element of this approach has been the objective of reducing the automatic growth ("crescimento
vegetativo") of the wage bill inherent inpay systems with bonusesbasedon seniority.

To aid the centralizing decisions for strategic establishment control, the government i s working to
improve its integrated personnel data system (SISAP). Some of the SISAP modules are not fully
operational yet, but the Government i s working toward the system's full operationalization during
2006. Installation of a new payment module that is integrated with SISAP would be a condition for
a second-tranche release.

Another key pillar of the MG government's human resource management reforms is to enhance the
merit base and performance orientations of the public servants. The government has already
introduced a new career structure and established 14groupingsfor its civil servants, not only with
a view to developing merit-based human resource structures, but also to increase flexibility in
strategic management of human resources. Seventy percent of its staff has been re-assigned into
such groupings. Besides strengthening the merit principle in staff recruitment, the new careers
would provide clear incentives for both aspirants to the public service and those who are already
working for the public administration, allow the government to invest in capacity building of the
existing staff, and, because of its more flexible design, let the government re-deploy staff more
easily than in the past according to its changing needs and priorities.

2) Efficiency and economy in logistics and supply management

InJanuary 2004 the state government launched a new IT system named SIAD - SistemaIntegrado
de Administra@o de Bens e ServiGos - which has been a key component in the state's efforts to
control expenditures, decrease waste, and improve value for money. Unit costs for supplies have
been cut (in the case of certain medical supplies by up to 50 percent)28through cross-checking
prices and tighter controls over procurement.         Overall the average economy on government
purchases has been of the order of 18 percent. The system now covers more than 90 percent of
purchases.

The government intends to expand the use of the registration of prices method more widely. This
i s a method, authorized in the federal procurement law but i s rarely used in practice, whereby the
government can solicit price proposals from would-be suppliers without having to commit itself to
purchasing their service at the time of the bidding. The government intends to operationalize this
method through installation of a SIAD module for the use of electronic registration of prices in
government purchases through the pregzo modality (reverse auction). It also plans to make a new
SIAD vehicle registrationmoduleto all the 16 state secretariats so that the fleets of motor vehicles,
an important segment of the state's physical assets, are efficiently managed.

3) Installation of results-based management

The core of the MG government's results-based management initiative has been the creation of
GERAESProgram as an operational framework for strategic management of government priorities
through the 31 "structural projects."     These projects receive preferential treatments in terms of
management attention and budgetary allocations. In 2005, the government defined and agreed on
pelformance goals and benchmarksfor all 31 of the "structural projects" and entered this data in

  they seek.      The portal's user-friendliness i s enhanced with the organization of access to

  driver's license", "Paying the IPVA" to make information search easier and faster.

C.      Promotion of Private Sector Development

1) Enhancement of the investment climate

Many of the reforms describedin earlier sections representcontributions to the investment climate;
for example, e-government and procurement reform, and the elimination of arrears from the public
accounts all bring benefits to the private sector. In addition, the following specific reforms have
strengthened the business environment inMinas.

First, the state government has initiated a set of streamlined processes for entrepreneurswishing to
start a business, including linkages to investment promotion, advisory, credit, and training services
provided by the state for businesses wishing to invest there.           These measures have been
implementedunder the umbrella project Facilita Minus, which includes a special line of credit for
small and micro enterprises and a "one-stop shop" (Minus Fricil) for registering new firms.

Second, Simples Minus, a program initiated in January 2005, provides micro, small and medium
enterprises with differentiated tax administration systems more appropriate to their needs,
dispensing with the need for onerous paperwork, reducing the number of procedures, and
simplifying tax calculations.

Third, adoption of a streamlined environmental licensing procedure with the use of decentralized
regional offices and web-based applications for environmental licenses simplifies private firms'
investment decisions.    Minas Gerais i s a national leader in decentralizing, automating, and
enhancing its system of environmental licensing.           Environmental licensing has now been
streamlined into a "one-stop shop" operation, in the sense that companies registering for licensing
of any activity submit a single request to the Secretariat of Environment and Sustainable
Development (SEMAD)and receive guidance on the whole environmental licensing procedure.

The establishment of Facilita Minus, Simples Minus, and the new environmental licensing
procedure constitute the conditions for the first-tranche release. For the second tranche, the
government intends to deepen these reforms by integrating the registry of taxpayers of the state
value-added tax (ICMS)in the Integrated RevenueAdministration System (SIARE)with the registry
of suppliers in SZAD so that a firm interested in bidding for government procurement would not
have to contact the Finance Secretariat separately to obtain the proof of no ICMS tax debt.

2) Public-privatepartnership

Minas Gerais was the first Brazilian state to pass a PPP law (Law 14.868, of December 16, 2003:
see Annex 7). Management structures are already in place within the Secretariat for Economic
Development (with grant financing form DB), financial guarantee mechanisms are being
developed, and the modeling of the first five projects i s now underway. PPP i s viewed by the state
government as an innovative means to provide core infrastructure services with emphasis on roads
and sanitation, improve public service delivery, and reduce regionaldisparities.



(EPM) solution for monitoring the projects and for controlling the main functions of the planning and
budgetingprocesses.


                                                                                                  27

The Minas PPP Law, like its federal counterpart, establishes the legal and administrative
framework within which PPP can occur. Key elements of the new framework include the creation
of guarantee mechanisms, with the participation of a state company (CODEMIG: Companhia de
Desenvolvimento do Estado de Minas Gerais) as a contractual counterpart and the holder of assets
to finance the payment of private-sector partner^.^'

The Minas law is in some aspects more restrictive than the federal law (or that of S5o Paulo,
another state with its own PPP law): projects must appear in the state multiyear investment plan
(PPAG), and PPP cannot cover, for example, the simple sub-contracting of public-service delivery
labor costs. Projects must also cover a period of at least five years and be for a value in excess of
R$20 million. These restrictions are designed to reduce the risk of PPP becoming a source of
significant future fiscal risk, through its overuse in cases where other modes of public contracting
are already appropriate. Finally, and perhaps most important, the law requires that for all PPP
contracts, prior technical studies clearly establish cost-benefit gains of PPP in comparison with the
alternative of public provision, to ensure that PPP improves the efficiency of the use of public
resourcesby the state government.

For the purpose of loan disbursement, the prior actions for the first tranche would be the approval
by the Minas Gerais legislative assembly of the state PPP Law and the creation of the specialized
PPP unit within the Secretariat of Economic Development. Prior to the second tranche release, the
government plans to conclude the design of a PPP guaranteemechanism

D. BANK FINANCIALASSISTANCEAND DISBURSEMENT CONDITIONS

The Bank's financial assistance in support of the program would be comprised of a two-tranche
loan for the total amount of US$170 million, divided between the first tranche of US$lOO million
and the second tranche of US$70 million. Given the State of Minas' fiscal and other policy
performance prior to the arrival of the current administration, the most remarkable achievement so
far was to successfully carry out the initial set of reforms including deep fiscal adjustments that
resulted in an impressive turnaround of its fiscal balance and the simultaneous launching of one of
the most comprehensive and coherently designed public management reform initiatives within the
Brazilian federation today. These reforms requireda number of legal reforms as well as technically
sound administrative reforms, which are not frequently observed among Brazilian states. The
second tranche would support continuation and incremental improvements of these efforts in
selectedpolicy areas.32Given the state's track record of reformimplementation so far, the risk that
the slight front-loading will dilute the government's incentive to carry out the remaining reforms i s
considered minimal (see the Risk section below).

The first tranche would be disbursed upon loan effectiveness in recognition of the significant legal
andadministrative reforms, described above and inAnnex 2, which the State Government of Minas
Gerais has already undertaken.




31The state law does not create the legal contract per se, since this is constitutionally within the aegis of

32In fact, the loan preparation was delayed considerably because of the procedural complexity surrounding

The second tranche would be released once the State Government met all the tranche release
conditions specified in the Annex 2 (Operations Policy Matrix, the fourth column "Next Steps

         (a) satisfactory overall Program implementation by the Borrower;

         (b) maintenance by the Guarantor of a macroeconomic policy framework that does not
jeopardize the objectives of the program; and

         (c) maintenance by the Borrower of an expenditure program and fiscal arrangements with
the Guarantor that are consistent with the objectives of the Program.

E. LINKTO CAS

The 2003 Brazil CAS for 2004-07 lays out a vision of a more equitable, sustainable, competitive
           The CAS also states: "building on progress over the past year, the Bank will continue its
ongoing relationship with sub-national governments, working in a more coordinated and integrated
fashion in the states and municipalities where it i s already active. Going forward, the Bank will
selectively lend to creditworthy states. State strategies, based on intensive dialogue with the state's
economic team on development priorities and goals, focus on the full array of challenges and
include policy reviews, public expenditure and other economic analysis, and fiduciary reviews and
relatedcapacity building, as well as other partners' activities in the state."

Inthe present context it is worth emphasizing two aspects of World Bank support to Minas Gerais.
First, the motivation which underlies the above paragraph is that state governments are, inpractice,
responsible for executing many areas of public policy in Brazil, from public safety to education and
health to export promotion. Bank support of effective public management at the state level i s
therefore fundamental for impact on the ground. This already constitutes a core of the Bank's
public sector governance strategy in Brazil, where the Bank supports strengthening of public sector
management through a series of integrated state and municipal investment operation^.^^          Second,
the Fiscal Responsibility Law and the system of checks and balances on state fiscal performance
has been a mainstay of Brazilian macroeconomic improvement in recent years. Bank support to
select states may therefore contribute to Brazil's development by strengthening this system.

The proposed strategy of support to Minas Gerais i s linked specifically to the CAS objectives of
strengthening macroeconomic and good governance foundations and competitiveness agenda in
Brazil. For the "foundations" the CAS identified three long-term goals: (i) macroeconomic
                                                                                 sound
management and fiscal reform; (ii)    more efficient public expenditure management; and (iii)      good
governance. All three are directly related to the objectives of the proposed operation, although
some of the medium-term outcomes that are thought to contribute to these long-term goals are not
touched upon in the proposed operation. For the "competitive Brazil pillar", the CAS listed the
following long-term goals: (i)    improved infrastructure; (ii)  broader and more efficient financial
sector; (iii)improved investment climate and competition; and (iv) more modem innovation policy.

Furthermore, the proposed operation's support for the state government's results-based
management is in line with a key objective of the 2003 Results-basedCAS, which stated that "the
Bank would assist governments at the federal and subnational levels to strengthen their
